					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-subjectpageheader">
</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Idea that small causes can have large effects</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Butterfly_effect_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Butterfly effect (disambiguation)">Butterfly effect (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><span><video id="mwe_player_0" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Double_pendulum_simultaneous_realisations.ogv/330px--Double_pendulum_simultaneous_realisations.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" data-mw-tmh="" class="mw-file-element" width="300" height="169" data-durationhint="220" data-mwtitle="Double_pendulum_simultaneous_realisations.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" resource="/wiki/File:Double_pendulum_simultaneous_realisations.ogv"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/4/44/Double_pendulum_simultaneous_realisations.ogv/Double_pendulum_simultaneous_realisations.ogv.480p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="480p.vp9.webm" data-width="854" data-height="480" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/4/44/Double_pendulum_simultaneous_realisations.ogv/Double_pendulum_simultaneous_realisations.ogv.1080p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="1080p.vp9.webm" data-width="1920" data-height="1080" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/44/Double_pendulum_simultaneous_realisations.ogv" type="video/ogg; codecs=&quot;theora&quot;" data-width="1920" data-height="1080" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/4/44/Double_pendulum_simultaneous_realisations.ogv/Double_pendulum_simultaneous_realisations.ogv.144p.mjpeg.mov" type="video/quicktime" data-transcodekey="144p.mjpeg.mov" data-width="256" data-height="144" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/4/44/Double_pendulum_simultaneous_realisations.ogv/Double_pendulum_simultaneous_realisations.ogv.240p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="240p.vp9.webm" data-width="426" data-height="240" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/4/44/Double_pendulum_simultaneous_realisations.ogv/Double_pendulum_simultaneous_realisations.ogv.360p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp8, vorbis&quot;" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" /></video></span><figcaption>Experimental demonstration of the butterfly effect with six recordings of the same <a href="/wiki/Double_pendulum" title="Double pendulum">double pendulum</a>. In each recording, the pendulum starts with almost the same initial condition. Over time, the differences in the dynamics grow from almost unnoticeable to drastic.</figcaption></figure>

</tr><tr><th class="sidebar-heading">
<span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Nuvola_apps_edu_mathematics_blue-p.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Nuvola_apps_edu_mathematics_blue-p.svg/20px-Nuvola_apps_edu_mathematics_blue-p.svg.png" decoding="async" width="20" height="20" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Nuvola_apps_edu_mathematics_blue-p.svg/40px-Nuvola_apps_edu_mathematics_blue-p.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="128" data-file-height="128" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Mathematics" title="Portal:Mathematics">Mathematics Portal</a></th></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333133064" /><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1239400231">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}@media(prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}}@media print{.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:none!important}}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Math_topics_sidebar" title="Template:Math topics sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Math_topics_sidebar" title="Template talk:Math topics sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Template:Math_topics_sidebar" title="Special:EditPage/Template:Math topics sidebar"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Chaos_theory" title="Chaos theory">chaos theory</a>, the <b>butterfly effect</b> is the sensitive dependence on <a href="/wiki/Initial_condition" title="Initial condition">initial conditions</a> in which a small change in one state of a <a href="/wiki/Deterministic_system" title="Deterministic system">deterministic</a> <a href="/wiki/Nonlinear_system" title="Nonlinear system">nonlinear system</a> can result in large differences in a later state.
</p><p>The term is closely associated with the work of the mathematician and meteorologist <a href="/wiki/Edward_Norton_Lorenz" title="Edward Norton Lorenz">Edward Norton Lorenz</a>. He noted that the butterfly effect is derived from the example of the details of a <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">tornado</a> (the exact time of formation, the exact path taken) being influenced by minor perturbations such as a distant <a href="/wiki/Butterfly" title="Butterfly">butterfly</a> flapping its wings several weeks earlier. Lorenz originally used a seagull causing a storm but was persuaded to make it more poetic with the use of a butterfly and tornado by 1972.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He discovered the effect when he observed runs of his <a href="/wiki/Numerical_weather_prediction" title="Numerical weather prediction">weather model</a> with initial condition data that were rounded in a seemingly inconsequential manner. He noted that the weather model would fail to reproduce the results of runs with the unrounded initial condition data. A very small change in initial conditions had created a significantly different outcome.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The idea that small causes may have large effects in weather was earlier acknowledged by the French mathematician and physicist <a href="/wiki/Henri_Poincar%C3%A9" title="Henri Poincaré">Henri Poincaré</a>. The American mathematician and philosopher <a href="/wiki/Norbert_Wiener" title="Norbert Wiener">Norbert Wiener</a> also contributed to this theory. Lorenz's work placed the concept of <i>instability</i> of the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">Earth's atmosphere</a> onto a quantitative base and linked the concept of instability to the properties of large classes of dynamic systems which are undergoing <a href="/wiki/Nonlinear_dynamics" class="mw-redirect" title="Nonlinear dynamics">nonlinear dynamics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chaos_theory" title="Chaos theory">deterministic chaos</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-scholarpedia&#95;4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scholarpedia-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The concept of the butterfly effect has since been used outside the context of weather science as a broad term for any situation where a small change is supposed to be the cause of larger consequences.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Butterfly_effect&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Chaos_theory#A_popular_but_inaccurate_analogy_for_chaos" title="Chaos theory">Chaos theory §&#160;A popular but inaccurate analogy for chaos</a></div>
<p>In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Vocation_of_Man" title="The Vocation of Man">The Vocation of Man</a></i> (1800), <a href="/wiki/Johann_Gottlieb_Fichte" title="Johann Gottlieb Fichte">Johann Gottlieb Fichte</a> says "you could not remove a single grain of sand from its place without thereby ... changing something throughout all parts of the immeasurable whole".
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Chaos_theory" title="Chaos theory">Chaos theory</a> and the sensitive dependence on initial conditions were described in numerous forms of literature. This is evidenced by the case of the <a href="/wiki/Three-body_problem" title="Three-body problem">three-body problem</a> by Poincaré in 1890.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-wolframscience.com&#95;5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wolframscience.com-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He later proposed that such phenomena could be common, for example, in meteorology.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 1898, <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Hadamard" title="Jacques Hadamard">Jacques Hadamard</a> noted general divergence of trajectories in spaces of negative curvature. <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Duhem" title="Pierre Duhem">Pierre Duhem</a> discussed the possible general significance of this in 1908.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-wolframscience.com&#95;5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wolframscience.com-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 1950, <a href="/wiki/Alan_Turing" title="Alan Turing">Alan Turing</a> noted: "The displacement of a single electron by a billionth of a centimetre at one moment might make the difference between a man being killed by an avalanche a year later, or escaping."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-turing1950&#95;7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-turing1950-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The idea that the death of one butterfly could eventually have a far-reaching <a href="/wiki/Ripple_effect" title="Ripple effect">ripple effect</a> on subsequent historical events made its earliest known appearance in "<a href="/wiki/A_Sound_of_Thunder" title="A Sound of Thunder">A Sound of Thunder</a>", a 1952 short story by <a href="/wiki/Ray_Bradbury" title="Ray Bradbury">Ray Bradbury</a> in which a time traveller alters the future by inadvertently treading on a butterfly in the past.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>More precisely, though, almost the exact idea and the exact phrasing —of a tiny insect's wing affecting the entire atmosphere's winds— was published in a children's book which became extremely successful and well-known globally in 1962, the year before Lorenz published:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1244412712">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 32px}.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;margin-top:0}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{padding-left:1.6em}}</style><blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>"...whatever we do affects everything and everyone else, if even in the tiniest way. Why, when a housefly flaps his wings, a breeze goes round the world."
</p><p>
-- The Princess of Pure Reason </p></blockquote><div class="templatequotecite"><p style="display: inline; padding-left: 2.3em;">—&#8202;Norton Juster, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Phantom_Tollbooth" title="The Phantom Tollbooth">The Phantom Tollbooth</a></i><span style="display:none" data-plural="1"></span></p></div>
<p>In 1961, Lorenz was running a numerical computer model to redo a weather prediction from the middle of the previous run as a shortcut. He entered the initial condition 0.506 from the printout instead of entering the full precision 0.506127 value. The result was a completely different weather scenario.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Lorenz wrote:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1244412712" /><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>
At one point I decided to repeat some of the computations in order to examine what was happening in greater detail. I stopped the computer, typed in a line of numbers that it had printed out a while earlier, and set it running again. I went down the hall for a cup of coffee and returned after about an hour, during which time the computer had simulated about two months of weather. The numbers being printed were nothing like the old ones. I immediately suspected a weak <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_tube" title="Vacuum tube">vacuum tube</a> or some other computer trouble, which was not uncommon, but before calling for service I decided to see just where the mistake had occurred, knowing that this could speed up the servicing process. Instead of a sudden break, I found that the new values at first repeated the old ones, but soon afterward differed by one and then several units in the last [decimal] place, and then began to differ in the next to the last place and then in the place before that. In fact, the differences more or less steadily doubled in size every four days or so, until all resemblance with the original output disappeared somewhere in the second month. This was enough to tell me what had happened: the numbers that I had typed in were not the exact original numbers, but were the rounded-off values that had appeared in the original printout. The initial round-off errors were the culprits; they were steadily amplifying until they dominated the solution. </p></blockquote><div class="templatequotecite"><p style="display: inline; padding-left: 2.3em;">—&#8202;E. N. Lorenz, <i>The Essence of Chaos</i>, University of Washington Press, Seattle (1993), page 134<sup id="cite&#95;ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><span style="display:none" data-plural="1"></span></p></div>
<p>In 1963, Lorenz published a theoretical study of this effect in a highly cited, seminal paper called <i>Deterministic Nonperiodic Flow</i><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> (the calculations were performed on a <a href="/wiki/Royal_McBee" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal McBee">Royal McBee</a> <a href="/wiki/LGP-30" title="LGP-30">LGP-30</a> computer).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Lorenz1963&#95;13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lorenz1963-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Elsewhere he stated:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1244412712" /><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>One meteorologist remarked that if the theory were correct, one flap of a <a href="/wiki/Gull" title="Gull">sea gull's</a> wings would be enough to alter the course of the weather forever. The controversy has not yet been settled, but the most recent evidence seems to favor the sea gulls.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Lorenz1963&#95;13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lorenz1963-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Polydamas_swallowtail_(Battus_polydamas_polydamas).JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Polydamas_swallowtail_%28Battus_polydamas_polydamas%29.JPG/250px-Polydamas_swallowtail_%28Battus_polydamas_polydamas%29.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Polydamas_swallowtail_%28Battus_polydamas_polydamas%29.JPG/500px-Polydamas_swallowtail_%28Battus_polydamas_polydamas%29.JPG 1.5x" data-file-width="2027" data-file-height="1352" /></a><figcaption>A <i><a href="/wiki/Battus_polydamas" title="Battus polydamas">Battus polydamas</a></i> butterfly in Brazil</figcaption></figure>
<p>Following proposals from colleagues, in later speeches and papers, Lorenz used the more poetic <a href="/wiki/Butterfly" title="Butterfly">butterfly</a>. According to Lorenz, when he failed to provide a title for a talk he was to present at the 139th meeting of the <a href="/wiki/American_Association_for_the_Advancement_of_Science" title="American Association for the Advancement of Science">American Association for the Advancement of Science</a> in 1972, Philip Merilees concocted <i>Does the flap of a butterfly's wings in Brazil set off a tornado in Texas?</i> as a title.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Although a butterfly flapping its wings has remained constant in the expression of this concept, the location of the butterfly, the consequences, and the location of the consequences have varied widely.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The phrase refers to the effect of a butterfly's wings creating tiny changes in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">atmosphere</a> that may ultimately alter the path of a <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">tornado</a> or delay, accelerate, or even prevent the occurrence of a tornado in another location. The butterfly does not power or directly create the tornado, but the term is intended to imply that the flap of the butterfly's wings can <i>cause</i> the tornado: in the sense that the flap of the wings is a part of the initial conditions of an interconnected complex web; one set of conditions leads to a tornado, while the other set of conditions doesn't. The flapping wing creates a small change in the initial condition of the system, which cascades to large-scale alterations of events (compare: <a href="/wiki/Domino_effect" title="Domino effect">domino effect</a>). Had the butterfly not flapped its wings, the <a href="/wiki/Trajectory" title="Trajectory">trajectory</a> of the system might have been vastly different—but it's also equally possible that the set of conditions without the butterfly flapping its wings is the set that leads to a tornado.
</p><p>The butterfly effect presents an obvious challenge to prediction, since initial conditions for a system such as the weather can never be known to complete accuracy. This problem motivated the development of <a href="/wiki/Ensemble_forecasting" title="Ensemble forecasting">ensemble forecasting</a>, in which a number of forecasts are made from perturbed initial conditions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Some scientists have since argued that the weather system is not as sensitive to initial conditions as previously believed.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/David_Orrell" title="David Orrell">David Orrell</a> argues that the major contributor to weather forecast error is model error, with sensitivity to initial conditions playing a relatively small role.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Wolfram" title="Stephen Wolfram">Stephen Wolfram</a> also notes that the <a href="/wiki/Lorenz_system" title="Lorenz system">Lorenz equations</a> are highly simplified and do not contain terms that represent viscous effects; he believes that these terms would tend to damp out small perturbations.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Recent studies using generalized <a href="/wiki/Lorenz_system" title="Lorenz system">Lorenz models</a> that included additional dissipative terms and nonlinearity suggested that a larger heating parameter is required for the onset of chaos.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>While the "butterfly effect" is often explained as being synonymous with sensitive dependence on initial conditions of the kind described by Lorenz in his 1963 paper (and previously observed by Poincaré), the butterfly metaphor was originally applied<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> to work he published in 1969<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which took the idea a step further. Lorenz proposed a mathematical model for how tiny motions in the atmosphere scale up to affect larger systems. He found that the systems in that model could only be predicted up to a specific point in the future, and beyond that, reducing the error in the initial conditions would not increase the predictability (as long as the error is not zero). This demonstrated that a deterministic system could be "observationally indistinguishable" from a non-deterministic one in terms of predictability. Recent re-examinations of this paper suggest that it offered a significant challenge to the idea that our universe is deterministic, comparable to the challenges offered by quantum physics.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3&#95;22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:4&#95;23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the book entitled <i>The Essence of Chaos</i> published in 1993,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:5&#95;24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Lorenz defined butterfly effect as: "The phenomenon that a small alteration in the state of a dynamical system will cause subsequent states to differ greatly from the states that would have followed without the alteration." This feature is the same as sensitive dependence of solutions on initial conditions (SDIC) in .<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the same book, Lorenz applied the activity of skiing and developed an idealized skiing model for revealing the sensitivity of time-varying paths to initial positions. A predictability horizon is determined before the onset of SDIC.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:7&#95;25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Theory_and_mathematical_definition">Theory and mathematical definition</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Butterfly_effect&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Theory and mathematical definition"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Chaos_theory#Lorenz&#39;s_pioneering_contributions_to_chaotic_modeling" title="Chaos theory">Chaos theory §&#160;Lorenz's pioneering contributions to chaotic modeling</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Lorenz_attractor_yb.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Lorenz_attractor_yb.svg/330px-Lorenz_attractor_yb.svg.png" decoding="async" width="310" height="310" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Lorenz_attractor_yb.svg/500px-Lorenz_attractor_yb.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Lorenz_attractor_yb.svg/960px-Lorenz_attractor_yb.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="750" /></a><figcaption>A plot of Lorenz' <a href="/wiki/Strange_attractor" class="mw-redirect" title="Strange attractor">strange attractor</a> for values ρ=28, σ = 10, β = 8/3. The butterfly effect or sensitive dependence on initial conditions is the property of a <a href="/wiki/Dynamical_system" title="Dynamical system">dynamical system</a> that, starting from any of various arbitrarily close alternative <a href="/wiki/Initial_condition" title="Initial condition">initial conditions</a> on the attractor, the <a href="/wiki/Iteration#Mathematics" title="Iteration">iterated points</a> will become arbitrarily spread out from each other.</figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Poincar%C3%A9_recurrence_theorem" title="Poincaré recurrence theorem">Recurrence</a>, the approximate return of a system toward its initial conditions, together with sensitive dependence on initial conditions, are the two main ingredients for chaotic motion. They have the practical consequence of making <a href="/wiki/Complex_system" title="Complex system">complex systems</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Weather" title="Weather">weather</a>, difficult to predict past a certain time range (approximately a week in the case of weather) since it is impossible to measure the starting atmospheric conditions completely accurately.
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Dynamical_system" title="Dynamical system">dynamical system</a> displays sensitive dependence on initial conditions if points arbitrarily close together separate over time at an exponential rate. The definition is not topological, but essentially metrical. Lorenz<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:5&#95;24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> defined sensitive dependence as follows:
</p><p><i>The property characterizing an orbit (i.e., a solution) if most other orbits that pass close to it at some point do not remain close to it as time advances.</i>
</p><p>If <i>M</i> is the <a href="/wiki/State_space_(dynamical_system)" class="mw-redirect" title="State space (dynamical system)">state space</a> for the map <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle f^{t}}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <msup>

          </mrow>
        </msup>

    </mrow>

</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle d(f^{\tau }(x),f^{\tau }(y))&gt;\mathrm {e} ^{a\tau }\,d(x,y)}">

<p>for some positive parameter <i>a</i>. The definition does not require that all points from a neighborhood separate from the base point <i>x</i>, but it requires one positive <a href="/wiki/Lyapunov_exponent" title="Lyapunov exponent">Lyapunov exponent</a>. In addition to a positive Lyapunov exponent, boundedness is another major feature within chaotic systems.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The simplest mathematical framework exhibiting sensitive dependence on initial conditions is provided by a particular parametrization of the <a href="/wiki/Logistic_map" title="Logistic map">logistic map</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle x_{n+1}=4x_{n}(1-x_{n}),\quad 0\leq x_{0}\leq 1,}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <msub>
          <mi>x</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mi>n</mi>
            <mo>+</mo>
            <mn>1</mn>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mn>4</mn>
        <msub>
          <mi>x</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mi>n</mi>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mn>1</mn>
        <mo>&#x2212;<!-- − --></mo>
        <msub>
          <mi>x</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mi>n</mi>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <mspace width="1em" />
        <mn>0</mn>

This solution equation clearly demonstrates the two key features of chaos – stretching and folding: the factor 2<sup><i>n</i></sup> shows the exponential growth of stretching, which results in sensitive dependence on initial conditions (the butterfly effect), while the squared sine function keeps <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle x_{n}}">

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="In_physical_systems">In physical systems</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Butterfly_effect&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: In physical systems"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="In_weather">In weather</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Butterfly_effect&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: In weather"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Overview">Overview</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Butterfly_effect&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Overview"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The butterfly effect is most familiar in terms of weather; it can easily be demonstrated in standard weather prediction models, for example. The climate scientists James Annan and William Connolley explain that chaos is important in the development of weather prediction methods; models are sensitive to initial conditions. They add the caveat: "Of course the existence of an unknown butterfly flapping its wings has no direct bearing on weather forecasts, since it will take far too long for such a small perturbation to grow to a significant size, and we have many more immediate uncertainties to worry about. So the direct impact of this phenomenon on weather prediction is often somewhat wrong."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Differentiating_types_of_butterfly_effects">Differentiating types of butterfly effects</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Butterfly_effect&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Differentiating types of butterfly effects"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The concept of the butterfly effect encompasses several phenomena. The two kinds of butterfly effects, including the sensitive dependence on initial conditions,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and the ability of a tiny perturbation to create an organized circulation at large distances,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> are not exactly the same.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In Palmer et al.,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3&#95;22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> a new type of butterfly effect is introduced, highlighting the potential impact of small-scale processes on finite predictability within the Lorenz 1969 model. Additionally, the identification of ill-conditioned aspects of the Lorenz 1969 model points to a practical form of finite predictability.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:7&#95;25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These two distinct mechanisms suggesting finite predictability in the Lorenz 1969 model are collectively referred to as the third kind of butterfly effect.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:8&#95;29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The authors in <sup id="cite&#95;ref-:8&#95;29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> have considered Palmer et al.'s suggestions and have aimed to present their perspective without raising specific contentions.
</p><p>The third kind of butterfly effect with finite predictability, as discussed in,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3&#95;22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> was primarily proposed based on a convergent geometric series, known as Lorenz's and Lilly's formulas. Ongoing discussions are addressing the validity of these two formulas for estimating predictability limits in.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A comparison of the two kinds of butterfly effects<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and the third kind of butterfly effect<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3&#95;22-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:4&#95;23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> has been documented.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:8&#95;29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In recent studies,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:7&#95;25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> it was reported that both meteorological and non-meteorological linear models have shown that instability plays a role in producing a butterfly effect, which is characterized by brief but significant exponential growth resulting from a small disturbance.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Recent_debates_on_butterfly_effects">Recent debates on butterfly effects</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Butterfly_effect&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Recent debates on butterfly effects"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The first kind of butterfly effect (BE1), known as SDIC (Sensitive Dependence on Initial Conditions), is widely recognized and demonstrated through idealized chaotic models. However, opinions differ regarding the second kind of butterfly effect, specifically the impact of a butterfly flapping its wings on tornado formation, as indicated in two 2024 articles.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:9&#95;32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:9-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In more recent discussions published by <i>Physics Today</i>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:10&#95;34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> it is acknowledged that the second kind of butterfly effect (BE2) has never been rigorously verified using a realistic weather model. While the studies suggest that BE2 is unlikely in the real atmosphere,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:9&#95;32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:9-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:10&#95;34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> its invalidity in this context does not negate the applicability of BE1 in other areas, such as pandemics or historical events.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>For the third kind of butterfly effect, the limited predictability within the Lorenz 1969 model is explained by scale interactions in one article<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3&#95;22-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and by system ill-conditioning in another more recent study.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:7&#95;25-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Finite_predictability_in_chaotic_systems">Finite predictability in chaotic systems</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Butterfly_effect&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Finite predictability in chaotic systems"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>According to Lighthill (1986),<sup id="cite&#95;ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the presence of SDIC (commonly known as the butterfly effect) implies that chaotic systems have a finite predictability limit. In a literature review,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> it was found that Lorenz's perspective on the predictability limit can be condensed into the following statement:
</p>
<ul><li>(A). The Lorenz 1963 model qualitatively revealed the essence of a finite predictability within a chaotic system such as the atmosphere. However, it did not determine a precise limit for the predictability of the atmosphere.</li>
<li>(B). In the 1960s, the two-week predictability limit was originally estimated based on a doubling time of five days in real-world models. Since then, this finding has been documented in Charney et al. (1966)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and has become a consensus.</li></ul>
<p>Recently, a short video has been created to present Lorenz's perspective on predictability limit.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A recent study refers to the two-week predictability limit, initially calculated in the 1960s with the Mintz-Arakawa model's five-day doubling time, as the "Predictability Limit Hypothesis."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Inspired by Moore's Law, this term acknowledges the collaborative contributions of Lorenz, Mintz, and Arakawa under Charney's leadership. The hypothesis supports the investigation into extended-range predictions using both partial differential equation (PDE)-based physics methods and Artificial Intelligence (AI) techniques.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="In_quantum_mechanics">In quantum mechanics</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Butterfly_effect&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: In quantum mechanics"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The potential for sensitive dependence on initial conditions (the butterfly effect) has been studied in a number of cases in <a href="/wiki/Semiclassical_physics" title="Semiclassical physics">semiclassical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum physics</a>, including atoms in strong fields and the anisotropic <a href="/wiki/Kepler_problem" title="Kepler problem">Kepler problem</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some authors have argued that extreme (exponential) dependence on initial conditions is not expected in pure quantum treatments;<sup id="cite&#95;ref-What&#95;is...&#95;Quantum&#95;Chaos&#95;45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-What_is..._Quantum_Chaos-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> however, the sensitive dependence on initial conditions demonstrated in classical motion is included in the semiclassical treatments developed by <a href="/wiki/Martin_Gutzwiller" title="Martin Gutzwiller">Martin Gutzwiller</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and John B. Delos and co-workers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The random matrix theory and simulations with quantum computers prove that some versions of the butterfly effect in quantum mechanics do not exist.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Other authors suggest that the butterfly effect can be observed in quantum systems. Zbyszek P. Karkuszewski et al. consider the time evolution of quantum systems which have slightly different <a href="/wiki/Hamiltonian_(quantum_mechanics)" title="Hamiltonian (quantum mechanics)">Hamiltonians</a>. They investigate the level of sensitivity of quantum systems to small changes in their given Hamiltonians.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> David Poulin et al. presented a quantum algorithm to measure fidelity decay, which "measures the rate at which identical initial states diverge when subjected to slightly different dynamics". They consider fidelity decay to be "the closest quantum analog to the (purely classical) butterfly effect".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Whereas the classical butterfly effect considers the effect of a small change in the position and/or velocity of an object in a given <a href="/wiki/Hamiltonian_system" title="Hamiltonian system">Hamiltonian system</a>, the quantum butterfly effect considers the effect of a small change in the Hamiltonian system with a given initial position and velocity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-iqc.ca&#95;52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iqc.ca-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This quantum butterfly effect has been demonstrated experimentally.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Quantum and semiclassical treatments of system sensitivity to initial conditions are known as <a href="/wiki/Quantum_chaos" title="Quantum chaos">quantum chaos</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-What&#95;is...&#95;Quantum&#95;Chaos&#95;45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-What_is..._Quantum_Chaos-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-iqc.ca&#95;52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iqc.ca-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Butterfly_effect&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: In popular culture"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Butterfly_effect_in_popular_culture" title="Butterfly effect in popular culture">Butterfly effect in popular culture</a></div><p>The butterfly effect has appeared across media such as literature (for instance, <i><a href="/wiki/A_Sound_of_Thunder" title="A Sound of Thunder">A Sound of Thunder</a></i>), films and television (such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Simpsons" title="The Simpsons">The Simpsons</a></i>), video games (such as <i><a href="/wiki/Life_Is_Strange" title="Life Is Strange">Life Is Strange</a></i>), webcomics (such as <i><a href="/wiki/Homestuck" title="Homestuck">Homestuck</a></i>), musical references (such as "Butterfly Effect" by <a href="/wiki/Travis_Scott" title="Travis Scott">Travis Scott</a>), AI-driven expansive language models, and more.
</p><div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="See_also">See also</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Butterfly_effect&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: See also"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
